<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Maize</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Cereal grain</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the cereal grain. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Maize_(disambiguation)" title="Maize (disambiguation)">Maize (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Corn" redirects here. For the band, see <a href="/wiki/Korn" title="Korn">Korn</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Corn_(disambiguation)" title="Corn (disambiguation)">Corn (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

</p>

<p><b>Maize</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">MAYZ</span></i></a>; <i>Zea mays</i> subsp. <i>mays</i>, from <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>: <i lang="es">maíz</i> after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taino_language" title="Taino language">Taino</a>: <i lang="tnq">mahiz</i>), also known as <b>corn</b>, is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cereal_grain" title="Cereal grain">cereal grain</a> first domesticated by <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">indigenous peoples</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> about 10,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The leafy stalk of the plant produces <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a> <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescences</a> and separate <a href="/wiki/Ovule" title="Ovule">ovuliferous</a> inflorescences called <a href="/wiki/Ear_(botany)" title="Ear (botany)">ears</a> that yield <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corn_kernels" title="Corn kernels">kernels</a> or seeds, which are <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruits</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Maize has become a <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> in many parts of the world, with the <a href="/wiki/List_of_most_valuable_crops_and_livestock_products" title="List of most valuable crops and livestock products">total production of maize surpassing</a> that of <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>. However, little of this maize is consumed directly by humans: most is used for <a href="/wiki/Corn_ethanol" title="Corn ethanol">corn ethanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animal_feed" title="Animal feed">animal feed</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Category:Maize_products" title="Category:Maize products">maize products</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Corn_starch" title="Corn starch">corn starch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corn_syrup" title="Corn syrup">corn syrup</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The six major types of maize are <a href="/wiki/Dent_corn" title="Dent corn">dent corn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flint_corn" title="Flint corn">flint corn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pod_corn" title="Pod corn">pod corn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Popcorn" title="Popcorn">popcorn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flour_corn" title="Flour corn">flour corn</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sweet_corn" title="Sweet corn">sweet corn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="620" data-file-width="800" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg/220px-Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg/330px-Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg/440px-Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guila_Naquitz_cave.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Guil%C3%A1_Naquitz_Cave" title="Guilá Naquitz Cave">Guilá Naquitz Cave</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">Oaxaca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> is the site of early <a href="/wiki/Domestication#plants" title="Domestication">domestication</a> of several food crops, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">teosinte</a> (an ancestor of maize).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benz_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-benz-5">[5]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Florentine_Codex-_Agriculture.tiff"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3593" data-file-width="2364" height="334" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/The_Florentine_Codex-_Agriculture.tiff/lossy-page1-220px-The_Florentine_Codex-_Agriculture.tiff.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/The_Florentine_Codex-_Agriculture.tiff/lossy-page1-330px-The_Florentine_Codex-_Agriculture.tiff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/The_Florentine_Codex-_Agriculture.tiff/lossy-page1-440px-The_Florentine_Codex-_Agriculture.tiff.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Florentine_Codex-_Agriculture.tiff" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cultivation of maize in an illustration from the 16th c. <a href="/wiki/Florentine_Codex" title="Florentine Codex">Florentine Codex</a></div></div></div>
<p>Most historians believe maize was domesticated in the <a href="/wiki/Tehuac%C3%A1n_Valley_matorral" title="Tehuacán Valley matorral">Tehuacán Valley</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Recent research in the early 21st century has modified this view somewhat; scholars now indicate the adjacent <a href="/wiki/Balsas_River" title="Balsas River">Balsas River</a> Valley of south-central Mexico as the center of domestication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>An influential 2002 study by Matsuoka <i>et al</i>. has demonstrated that, rather than the multiple independent domestications model, all maize arose from a single domestication in southern Mexico about 9,000 years ago. The study also demonstrated that the oldest surviving maize types are those of the Mexican highlands. Later, maize spread from this region over the Americas along two major paths. This is consistent with a model based on the archaeological record suggesting that maize diversified in the highlands of Mexico before spreading to the lowlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mat_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mat-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencenow_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciencenow-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archaeologist Dolores Piperno has said:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> A large corpus of data indicates that it [maize] was dispersed into lower Central America by 7600 BP [5600 BC] and had moved into the inter-Andean valleys of Colombia between 7000 and 6000 BP [5000–4000 BC]. </p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Dolores Piperno, <i>The Origins of Plant Cultivation and Domestication in the New World Tropics: Patterns, Process, and New Developments</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-7">[7]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Since then, even earlier dates have been published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pagán-JiménezGuachamín-Tello2015_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pagán-JiménezGuachamín-Tello2015-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a genetic study by <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Agricultural_Research_Corporation" title="Brazilian Agricultural Research Corporation">Embrapa</a>, corn cultivation was introduced in South America from Mexico, in two great waves: the first, more than 6000 years ago, spread through the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a>. Evidence of cultivation in Peru has been found dating to about 6700 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_Mundo_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Mundo-11">[11]</a></sup> The second wave, about 2000 years ago, through the lowlands of South America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before domestication, maize plants grew only small, 25 millimetres (1 in) long corn cobs, and only one per plant. In Spielvogel's view, many centuries of artificial selection (rather than the current view that maize was exploited by interplanting with <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">teosinte</a></i>) by the indigenous people of the Americas resulted in the development of maize plants capable of growing several cobs per plant, which were usually several centimetres/inches long each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spielvogel2005_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spielvogel2005-13">[13]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olmec" title="Olmec">Olmec</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maya_peoples" title="Maya peoples">Maya</a> cultivated maize in numerous varieties throughout <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>; they cooked, ground and processed it through <a href="/wiki/Nixtamalization" title="Nixtamalization">nixtamalization</a>. It was believed that beginning about 2500 BC, the crop spread through much of the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archsouth_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-archsouth-14">[14]</a></sup> Research of the 21st century has established even earlier dates. The region developed a trade network based on surplus and varieties of maize crops.
</p><p>Maize is the most widely grown grain <a href="/wiki/Crop" title="Crop">crop</a> throughout the Americas, with 361 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">metric tons</a> grown in the United States in 2014 (Production table). Approximately 40% of the crop—130 million tons—is used for corn <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_maize" title="Genetically modified maize">Genetically modified maize</a> made up 85% of the maize planted in the United States in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GMOCompass_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-GMOCompass-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sugar-rich varieties called <a href="/wiki/Sweet_corn" title="Sweet corn">sweet corn</a> are usually grown for human consumption as kernels, while <a href="/wiki/Field_corn" title="Field corn">field corn</a> varieties are used for animal feed, various corn-based human food uses (including grinding into <a href="/wiki/Cornmeal" title="Cornmeal">cornmeal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Masa" title="Masa">masa</a>, pressing into <a href="/wiki/Corn_oil" title="Corn oil">corn oil</a>, and fermentation and distillation into alcoholic beverages like <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_whiskey" title="Bourbon whiskey">bourbon whiskey</a>), and as chemical feedstocks.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Columbian_exchange">Columbian exchange</span></h3>
<p>After the arrival of Europeans in 1492, Spanish settlers consumed maize and explorers and traders <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">carried it back to Europe</a> and introduced it to other countries. Spanish settlers far preferred wheat bread to maize, <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">cassava</a>, or potatoes. Maize flour could not be substituted for wheat for communion bread, since in <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> belief only wheat could undergo <a href="/wiki/Transubstantiation" title="Transubstantiation">transubstantiation</a> and be transformed into the body of Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Some Spaniards worried that by eating indigenous foods, which they did not consider nutritious, they would weaken and risk  turning into Indians. "In the view of Europeans, it was the food they ate, even more than the environment in which they lived, that gave Amerindians and Spaniards both their distinctive physical characteristics and their characteristic personalities."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Despite these worries, Spaniards did consume maize. Archeological evidence from Florida sites indicate they cultivated it as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Maize spread to the rest of the world because of its ability to grow in diverse climates. It was cultivated in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> just a few decades after Columbus's voyages and then spread to <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, West Africa and elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corntassel_7095.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="800" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Corntassel_7095.jpg/170px-Corntassel_7095.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Corntassel_7095.jpg/255px-Corntassel_7095.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Corntassel_7095.jpg/340px-Corntassel_7095.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corntassel_7095.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Many small male flowers make up the male inflorescence, called the tassel.</div></div></div>
<p>The word <i>maize</i> derives from the Spanish form of the indigenous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ta%C3%ADno_people" title="Taíno people">Taíno</a> word for the plant, <i>mahiz</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> It is known by other names around the world.
</p><p>The word "corn" outside North America, Australia, and New Zealand refers to any <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereal</a> crop, its meaning understood to vary geographically to refer to the local <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_corn_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED_corn-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger-22">[22]</a></sup> In the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_corn_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED_corn-21">[21]</a></sup> Canada,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Australia, and New Zealand,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> <i>corn</i> primarily means maize; this usage started as a shortening of "Indian corn".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_corn_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-OED_corn-21">[21]</a></sup> "Indian corn" primarily means maize (the staple grain of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_Americans" title="Indigenous Americans">indigenous Americans</a>), but can refer more specifically to multicolored "<a href="/wiki/Flint_corn" title="Flint corn">flint corn</a>" used for decoration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>In places outside North America, Australia, and New Zealand, <i>corn</i> often refers to maize in culinary contexts. The narrower meaning is usually indicated by some additional word, as in <i><a href="/wiki/Sweet_corn" title="Sweet corn">sweet corn</a></i>, <i>sweetcorn</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Corn_on_the_cob" title="Corn on the cob">corn on the cob</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Baby_corn" title="Baby corn">baby corn</a></i>, the puffed confection known as <i><a href="/wiki/Popcorn" title="Popcorn">popcorn</a></i> and the breakfast cereal known as <i><a href="/wiki/Corn_flakes" title="Corn flakes">corn flakes</a></i>.
</p><p>In Southern Africa, maize is commonly called <i>mielie</i> (<a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">Afrikaans</a>) or <i>mealie</i> (<a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_mealie_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED_mealie-26">[26]</a></sup> words derived from the Portuguese word for maize, <i>milho</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Maize</i> is preferred in formal, scientific, and international usage because it refers specifically to this one grain, unlike <i>corn</i>, which has a complex variety of meanings that vary by context and geographic region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger-22">[22]</a></sup> <i>Maize</i> is used by agricultural bodies and <a href="/wiki/Research_institute" title="Research institute">research institutes</a> such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FAO" title="FAO">FAO</a> and <a href="/wiki/CSIRO" title="CSIRO">CSIRO</a>. National agricultural and industry associations often include the word <i>maize</i> in their name even in English-speaking countries where the local, informal word is something other than <i>maize</i>; for example, the Maize Association of Australia, the Indian Maize Development Association, the Kenya Maize Consortium and Maize Breeders Network, the National Maize Association of Nigeria, the Zimbabwe Seed Maize Association. However, in commodities trading, <i>corn</i> consistently refers to maize and not other grains.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_physiology">Structure and physiology</span></h2>
<p>The maize plant is often 3 m (10 ft) in height,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Races_of_Maize_in_Mexico_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Races_of_Maize_in_Mexico-28">[28]</a></sup> though some natural strains can grow 13 m (43 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The stem is commonly composed of 20 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internode_(botany)" title="Internode (botany)">internodes</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> of 18 cm (7.1 in) length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Races_of_Maize_in_Mexico_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Races_of_Maize_in_Mexico-28">[28]</a></sup>  A leaf, which grows from each node, is generally 9 cm (4 in) in width and 120 cm (4 ft) in length.
</p><p>Ears develop above a few of the leaves in the midsection of the plant, between the stem and leaf sheath, elongating by around 3 millimetres (0.12 in) per day, to a length of 18 cm (7 in) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Races_of_Maize_in_Mexico_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Races_of_Maize_in_Mexico-28">[28]</a></sup> with 60 cm (24 in) being the maximum alleged in the subspecies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>  They are female <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescences</a>, tightly enveloped by several layers of ear leaves commonly called husks. Certain varieties of maize have been bred to produce many additional developed ears. These are the source of the "<a href="/wiki/Baby_corn" title="Baby corn">baby corn</a>" used as a vegetable in <a href="/wiki/Asian_cuisine" title="Asian cuisine">Asian cuisine</a>.
</p><p>The apex of the stem ends in the tassel, an <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescence</a> of male flowers. When the tassel is mature and conditions are suitably warm and dry, anthers on the tassel <a href="/wiki/Dehiscence_(botany)" title="Dehiscence (botany)">dehisce</a> and release pollen. Maize pollen is <a href="/wiki/Anemophily" title="Anemophily">anemophilous</a> (dispersed by wind), and because of its large settling velocity, most pollen falls within a few meters of the tassel.
</p><p>Elongated <a href="/wiki/Stigma_(botany)" title="Stigma (botany)">stigmas</a>, called <a href="/wiki/Corn_silk" title="Corn silk">silks</a>, emerge from the whorl of husk leaves at the end of the ear. They are often pale yellow and 18 cm (7 in) in length, like tufts of hair in appearance. At the end of each is a carpel, which may develop into a "kernel" if fertilized by a pollen grain. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pericarp" title="Pericarp">pericarp</a> of the fruit is fused with the seed coat referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Caryopsis" title="Caryopsis">caryopsis</a>", typical of the <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">grasses</a>, and the entire kernel is often referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seed</a>". The cob is close to a <a href="/wiki/Multiple_fruit" title="Multiple fruit">multiple fruit</a> in structure, except that the individual fruits (the kernels) never fuse into a single mass. The grains are about the size of <a href="/wiki/Pea" title="Pea">peas</a>, and adhere in regular rows around a white, pithy substance, which forms the ear. The maximum size of kernels is reputedly 2.5 cm (1 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> An ear commonly holds 600 kernels. They are of various colors: blackish, <a href="/wiki/Blue_corn" title="Blue corn">bluish-gray</a>, <a href="/wiki/Purple_corn" title="Purple corn">purple</a>, green, red, white and yellow. When ground into <a href="/wiki/Flour" title="Flour">flour</a>, maize yields more flour with much less <a href="/wiki/Bran" title="Bran">bran</a> than wheat does. It lacks the protein <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a> of wheat and, therefore, makes baked goods with poor rising capability. A <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">genetic variant</a> that accumulates more sugar and less <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> in the ear is consumed as a vegetable and is called <a href="/wiki/Sweet_corn" title="Sweet corn">sweet corn</a>. Young ears can be consumed raw, with the <a href="/wiki/Corncob" title="Corncob">cob</a> and silk, but as the plant matures (usually during the summer months), the cob becomes tougher and the silk dries to inedibility. By the end of the <a href="/wiki/Growing_season" title="Growing season">growing season</a>, the kernels dry out and become difficult to chew without cooking them tender first in boiling water.
</p>

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 83.333333333333px"><div style="width: 83.333333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 81.333333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ZeaMays.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1356" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/ZeaMays.jpg/122px-ZeaMays.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/ZeaMays.jpg/183px-ZeaMays.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/ZeaMays.jpg/244px-ZeaMays.jpg 2x" width="82"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Full-grown maize plants
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 92px"><div style="width: 92px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 90px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Klip_kukuruza_uzgojen_u_Me%C4%91imurju_(Croatia).JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Klip_kukuruza_uzgojen_u_Me%C4%91imurju_%28Croatia%29.JPG/135px-Klip_kukuruza_uzgojen_u_Me%C4%91imurju_%28Croatia%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Klip_kukuruza_uzgojen_u_Me%C4%91imurju_%28Croatia%29.JPG/202px-Klip_kukuruza_uzgojen_u_Me%C4%91imurju_%28Croatia%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Klip_kukuruza_uzgojen_u_Me%C4%91imurju_%28Croatia%29.JPG/270px-Klip_kukuruza_uzgojen_u_Me%C4%91imurju_%28Croatia%29.JPG 2x" width="90"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Mature maize ear on a stalk
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Planting density affects multiple aspects of maize. Modern farming techniques in <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed countries</a> usually rely on dense planting, which produces one ear per stalk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Stands of <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">silage</a> maize are yet denser,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> and achieve a lower percentage of ears and more plant matter.
</p><p>Maize is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-night_plant" title="Long-night plant">facultative short-day plant</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and flowers in a certain number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Growing_degree_day" title="Growing degree day">growing degree days</a> &gt; 10 °C (50 °F) in the environment to which it is adapted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The magnitude of the influence that long nights have on the number of days that must pass before maize <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">flowers</a> is genetically prescribed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and regulated by the <a href="/wiki/Phytochrome" title="Phytochrome">phytochrome</a> system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Circadian_rhythm" title="Circadian rhythm">Photoperiodicity</a> can be eccentric in tropical <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> such that the long days characteristic of higher latitudes allow the plants to grow so tall that they do not have enough time to produce seed before being killed by frost. These attributes, however, may prove useful in using tropical maize for <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Immature maize shoots accumulate a powerful antibiotic substance, 2,4-dihydroxy-7-methoxy-1,4-benzoxazin-3-one (<a href="/wiki/DIMBOA" title="DIMBOA">DIMBOA</a>). DIMBOA is a member of a group of <a href="/wiki/Hydroxamic_acid" title="Hydroxamic acid">hydroxamic acids</a> (also known as benzoxazinoids) that serve as a natural defense against a wide range of pests, including insects, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pathogenic" title="Pathogenic">pathogenic</a> fungi and <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>. DIMBOA is also found in related grasses, particularly wheat. A maize mutant (bx) lacking DIMBOA is highly susceptible to attack by <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>. DIMBOA is also responsible for the relative resistance of immature maize to the <a href="/wiki/European_corn_borer" title="European corn borer">European corn borer</a> (family <a href="/wiki/Crambidae" title="Crambidae">Crambidae</a>). As maize matures, DIMBOA levels and resistance to the corn borer decline.
</p><p>Because of its shallow roots, maize is susceptible to droughts, intolerant of nutrient-deficient soils, and prone to be uprooted by severe winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 162px"><div style="width: 162px">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
