</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zea_mays_%27Ottofile_giallo_Tortonese%27_MHNT.BOT.2015.34.1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3238" data-file-width="5012" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Zea_mays_%27Ottofile_giallo_Tortonese%27_MHNT.BOT.2015.34.1.jpg/220px-Zea_mays_%27Ottofile_giallo_Tortonese%27_MHNT.BOT.2015.34.1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Zea_mays_%27Ottofile_giallo_Tortonese%27_MHNT.BOT.2015.34.1.jpg/330px-Zea_mays_%27Ottofile_giallo_Tortonese%27_MHNT.BOT.2015.34.1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Zea_mays_%27Ottofile_giallo_Tortonese%27_MHNT.BOT.2015.34.1.jpg/440px-Zea_mays_%27Ottofile_giallo_Tortonese%27_MHNT.BOT.2015.34.1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zea_mays_%27Ottofile_giallo_Tortonese%27_MHNT.BOT.2015.34.1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Zea mays 'Ottofile giallo Tortonese'</i> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MHNT" title="MHNT">MHNT</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zea_mays_fraise_MHNT.BOT.2011.18.21.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3294" data-file-width="4992" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Zea_mays_fraise_MHNT.BOT.2011.18.21.jpg/220px-Zea_mays_fraise_MHNT.BOT.2011.18.21.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Zea_mays_fraise_MHNT.BOT.2011.18.21.jpg/330px-Zea_mays_fraise_MHNT.BOT.2011.18.21.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Zea_mays_fraise_MHNT.BOT.2011.18.21.jpg/440px-Zea_mays_fraise_MHNT.BOT.2011.18.21.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zea_mays_fraise_MHNT.BOT.2011.18.21.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Zea mays "strawberry"—<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MHNT" title="MHNT">MHNT</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dent_Corn_%27Oaxacan_Green%27_(Zea_mays)_MHNT_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6590" data-file-width="7665" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Dent_Corn_%27Oaxacan_Green%27_%28Zea_mays%29_MHNT_2.jpg/220px-Dent_Corn_%27Oaxacan_Green%27_%28Zea_mays%29_MHNT_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Dent_Corn_%27Oaxacan_Green%27_%28Zea_mays%29_MHNT_2.jpg/330px-Dent_Corn_%27Oaxacan_Green%27_%28Zea_mays%29_MHNT_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Dent_Corn_%27Oaxacan_Green%27_%28Zea_mays%29_MHNT_2.jpg/440px-Dent_Corn_%27Oaxacan_Green%27_%28Zea_mays%29_MHNT_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dent_Corn_%27Oaxacan_Green%27_(Zea_mays)_MHNT_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Zea mays "Oaxacan Green"</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MHNT" title="MHNT">MHNT</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corncobs.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="667" data-file-width="1000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Corncobs.jpg/220px-Corncobs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Corncobs.jpg/330px-Corncobs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Corncobs.jpg/440px-Corncobs.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corncobs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Variegated maize ears</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1960" data-file-width="3008" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg/220px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg/330px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg/440px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CSIRO_ScienceImage_3195_Maize_or_corn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Multicolored corn kernels (<a href="/wiki/CSIRO" title="CSIRO">CSIRO</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>While yellow maizes derive their color from <a href="/wiki/Lutein" title="Lutein">lutein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zeaxanthin" title="Zeaxanthin">zeaxanthin</a>, in red-colored maizes, the kernel coloration is due to <a href="/wiki/Anthocyanin" title="Anthocyanin">anthocyanins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phlobaphene" title="Phlobaphene">phlobaphenes</a>. These latter substances are synthesized in the flavonoids synthetic pathway<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> from polymerisation of <a href="/wiki/Flavan-4-ol" title="Flavan-4-ol">flavan-4-ols</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> by the expression of maize pericarp color1 (p1) gene<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chopra2003_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chopra2003-43">[43]</a></sup> which encodes an R2R3 <a href="/wiki/MYB_(gene)" title="MYB (gene)">myb</a>-like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcriptional_activator" title="Transcriptional activator">transcriptional activator</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> of the A1 gene encoding for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dihydroflavonol_4-reductase" title="Dihydroflavonol 4-reductase">dihydroflavonol 4-reductase</a> (reducing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dihydroflavonol" title="Dihydroflavonol">dihydroflavonols</a> into flavan-4-ols)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> while another gene (Suppressor of Pericarp Pigmentation 1 or SPP1) acts as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcriptional_suppressor" title="Transcriptional suppressor">suppressor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The p1 gene encodes an Myb-homologous transcriptional activator of genes required for biosynthesis of red phlobaphene pigments, while the P1-wr allele specifies colorless kernel pericarp and red cobs, and unstable factor for orange1 (Ufo1) modifies P1-wr expression to confer pigmentation in kernel pericarp, as well as vegetative tissues, which normally do not accumulate significant amounts of phlobaphene pigments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chopra2003_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chopra2003-43">[43]</a></sup> The maize P gene encodes a Myb homolog that recognizes the sequence CCT/AACC, in sharp contrast with the C/TAACGG bound by vertebrate Myb proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abnormal_flowers">Abnormal flowers</span></h3>
<p>Maize flowers may sometimes exhibit mutations that lead to the formation of female flowers in the tassel. These mutations, <i>ts4</i> and <i>Ts6</i>, prohibit the development of the stamen while simultaneously promoting pistil development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> This may cause <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescences</a> containing both male and female flowers, or <a href="/wiki/Hermaphrodite" title="Hermaphrodite">hermaphrodite</a> flowers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GEM_corn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1864" data-file-width="2790" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/GEM_corn.jpg/220px-GEM_corn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/GEM_corn.jpg/330px-GEM_corn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/GEM_corn.jpg/440px-GEM_corn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GEM_corn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Exotic varieties of maize are collected to add <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> when selectively breeding new domestic strains</div></div></div>
<p>Maize is an annual grass in the family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gramineae" title="Gramineae">Gramineae</a>, which includes such plants as <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a>. There are two major species of the <a href="/wiki/Zea_(plant)" title="Zea (plant)">Zea</a> genus: <i>Zea mays</i> (maize) and Zea diploperennis, which is a <a href="/wiki/Perennial_plant" title="Perennial plant">perennial</a> type of teosinte. The <a href="/wiki/Annual_plant" title="Annual plant">annual</a> teosinte variety called <i>Zea mays mexicana</i> is the closest botanical relative to maize. It still grows in the wild as an annual in Mexico and Guatemala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oup_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-oup-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many forms of maize are used for food, sometimes classified as various subspecies related to the amount of starch each has:
</p>
<ul><li>Flour corn: <i>Zea mays </i>var.<i> amylacea</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Popcorn" title="Popcorn">Popcorn</a>: <i>Zea mays </i>var.<i> everta</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dent_corn" title="Dent corn">Dent corn</a> : <i>Zea mays </i>var.<i> indentata</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Flint_corn" title="Flint corn">Flint corn</a>: <i>Zea mays </i>var.<i> indurata</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sweet_corn" title="Sweet corn">Sweet corn</a>: <i>Zea mays </i>var.<i> saccharata</i> and <i>Zea mays</i> var. <i>rugosa</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Waxy_corn" title="Waxy corn">Waxy corn</a>: <i> Zea mays </i>var.<i> ceratina</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Amylomaize" title="Amylomaize">Amylomaize</a>: <i> Zea mays</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pod_corn" title="Pod corn">Pod corn</a>: <i>Zea mays </i>var.<i> tunicata</i> Larrañaga ex A. St. Hil.</li>
<li>Striped maize: <i>Zea mays </i>var.<i> japonica</i></li></ul>
<p>This system has been replaced (though not entirely displaced) over the last 60 years by multivariable classifications based on ever more data. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agronomics" title="Agronomics">Agronomic</a> data were supplemented by botanical traits for a robust initial classification, then genetic, <a href="/wiki/Cell_biology" title="Cell biology">cytological</a>, protein and DNA evidence was added. Now, the categories are forms (little used), races, racial complexes, and recently branches.
</p><p>Maize is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploid" title="Diploid">diploid</a> with 20 <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a> (n=10). The combined length of the chromosomes is 1500 <a href="/wiki/Centimorgan" title="Centimorgan">cM</a>. Some of the maize chromosomes have what are known as "chromosomal knobs": highly repetitive <a href="/wiki/Heterochromatin" title="Heterochromatin">heterochromatic</a> domains that stain darkly. Individual knobs are <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)" title="Polymorphism (biology)">polymorphic</a> among strains of both maize and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">teosinte</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Barbara_McClintock" title="Barbara McClintock">Barbara McClintock</a> used these knob markers to validate her <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transposon" title="Transposon">transposon</a> theory of "jumping genes", for which she won the 1983 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine</a>. Maize is still an important <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organism</a> for genetics and <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">developmental biology</a> today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Maize Genetics Cooperation Stock Center, funded by the USDA <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_Research_Service" title="Agricultural Research Service">Agricultural Research Service</a> and located in the Department of Crop Sciences at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Illinois_at_Urbana-Champaign" title="University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign">University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign</a>, is a stock center of maize mutants. The total collection has nearly 80,000 samples. The bulk of the collection consists of several hundred named genes, plus additional gene combinations and other heritable variants. There are about 1000 chromosomal aberrations (e.g., translocations and inversions) and stocks with abnormal chromosome numbers (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetraploid" title="Tetraploid">tetraploids</a>). Genetic data describing the maize mutant stocks as well as myriad other data about maize genetics can be accessed at <a class="external text" href="http://maizegdb.org/" rel="nofollow">MaizeGDB</a>, the Maize Genetics and Genomics Database.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2005, the US <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a> (NSF), Department of Agriculture (<a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">USDA</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">Department of Energy</a> (DOE) formed a consortium to sequence the B73 maize <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a>. The resulting DNA sequence data was deposited immediately into <a href="/wiki/GenBank" title="GenBank">GenBank</a>, a public repository for genome-sequence data. Sequences and genome annotations have also been made available throughout the project's lifetime at the project's official site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Primary sequencing of the maize genome was completed in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> On November 20, 2009, the consortium published results of its sequencing effort in <i>Science</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The genome, 85% of which is composed of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transposon" title="Transposon">transposons</a>, was found to contain 32,540 genes (By comparison, the <a href="/wiki/Human_genome" title="Human genome">human genome</a> contains about 2.9 billion bases and 26,000 genes). Much of the maize genome has been duplicated and reshuffled by <a href="/wiki/Helitron_(biology)" title="Helitron (biology)">helitrons</a>—group of <a href="/wiki/Rolling_circle_replication" title="Rolling circle replication">rolling circle</a> transposons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Field,_corn,_Liechtenstein,_Mountains,_Alps,_Vaduz,_sky,_clouds,_landscape.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Field%2C_corn%2C_Liechtenstein%2C_Mountains%2C_Alps%2C_Vaduz%2C_sky%2C_clouds%2C_landscape.jpg/220px-Field%2C_corn%2C_Liechtenstein%2C_Mountains%2C_Alps%2C_Vaduz%2C_sky%2C_clouds%2C_landscape.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Field%2C_corn%2C_Liechtenstein%2C_Mountains%2C_Alps%2C_Vaduz%2C_sky%2C_clouds%2C_landscape.jpg/330px-Field%2C_corn%2C_Liechtenstein%2C_Mountains%2C_Alps%2C_Vaduz%2C_sky%2C_clouds%2C_landscape.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Field%2C_corn%2C_Liechtenstein%2C_Mountains%2C_Alps%2C_Vaduz%2C_sky%2C_clouds%2C_landscape.jpg/440px-Field%2C_corn%2C_Liechtenstein%2C_Mountains%2C_Alps%2C_Vaduz%2C_sky%2C_clouds%2C_landscape.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Field,_corn,_Liechtenstein,_Mountains,_Alps,_Vaduz,_sky,_clouds,_landscape.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Field of maize in <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a></div></div></div>
<p>Maize reproduces sexually each year. This randomly selects half the genes from a given plant to propagate to the next generation, meaning that desirable traits found in the crop (like high yield or good nutrition) can be lost in subsequent generations unless certain techniques are used.
</p><p>Maize breeding in prehistory resulted in large plants producing large ears. Modern breeding began with individuals who selected highly productive varieties in their fields and then sold seed to other farmers. James L. Reid was one of the earliest and most successful developing Reid's Yellow Dent in the 1860s. These early efforts were based on <a href="/wiki/Selection_methods_in_plant_breeding_based_on_mode_of_reproduction" title="Selection methods in plant breeding based on mode of reproduction">mass selection</a>. Later breeding efforts included ear to row selection (C. G. Hopkins c. 1896), hybrids made from selected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inbred" title="Inbred">inbred</a> lines (G. H. Shull, 1909), and the highly successful double cross hybrids using four inbred lines (<a href="/wiki/Donald_F._Jones" title="Donald F. Jones">D. F. Jones</a> c. 1918, 1922). University supported breeding programs were especially important in developing and introducing modern hybrids (Ref Jugenheimer Hybrid Maize Breeding and Seed Production pub. 1958). By the 1930s, companies such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pioneer_Hi-Bred" title="Pioneer Hi-Bred">Pioneer</a> devoted to production of hybrid maize had begun to influence long term development. Internationally important seed banks such as the <a href="/wiki/International_Maize_and_Wheat_Improvement_Center" title="International Maize and Wheat Improvement Center">International Maize and Wheat Improvement Center</a> (CIMMYT) and the US bank at the Maize Genetics Cooperation Stock Center <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Illinois_at_Urbana-Champaign" title="University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign">University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign</a> maintain <a href="/wiki/Germplasm" title="Germplasm">germplasm</a> important for future crop development.
</p><p>Since the 1940s the best strains of maize have been first-generation hybrids made from inbred strains that have been optimized for specific traits, such as yield, nutrition, drought, pest and disease tolerance. Both conventional cross-breeding and genetic modification have succeeded in increasing output and reducing the need for cropland, pesticides, water and fertilizer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt14_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt14-57">[57]</a></sup> There is conflicting evidence to support the hypothesis that maize yield potential has increased over the past few decades. This suggests that changes in yield potential are associated with leaf angle, lodging resistance, tolerance of high plant density, disease/pest tolerance, and other agronomic traits rather than increase of yield potential per individual plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_maize_program">Global maize program</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2323" data-file-width="10072" height="51" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg/220px-2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg/330px-2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg/440px-2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2014_Panorama_Chiang_Klang_District.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Panorama of cornfields in <a href="/wiki/Nan_Province" title="Nan Province">Nan Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a></div></div></div>
<p>CIMMYT operates a conventional breeding program to provide optimized strains. The program began in the 1980s. Hybrid seeds are distributed in Africa by the Drought Tolerant Maize for Africa project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt14_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt14-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_modification">Genetic modification</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transgenic_maize" title="Transgenic maize">Transgenic maize</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetically_modified" title="Genetically modified">Genetically modified</a> <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_maize" title="Genetically modified maize">(GM) maize</a> was one of the 26 <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">GM crops</a> grown commercially in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Grown since 1997 in the United States and Canada, 92% of the US maize crop was genetically modified in 2016<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> and 33% of the worldwide maize crop was GM in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> As of 2011, Herbicide-tolerant maize varieties were grown in Argentina, Australia, Brazil, Canada, China, Colombia, El Salvador, the European Union, Honduras, Japan, Korea, Malaysia, Mexico, New Zealand, Philippines, the Russian Federation, Singapore, South Africa, Taiwan, Thailand, and USA, and insect-resistant corn was grown in Argentina, Australia, Brazil, Canada, Chile, China, Colombia, Czech Republic, Egypt, the EU, Honduras, Japan, Korea, Malaysia, Mexico, Netherlands, New Zealand, Philippines, Romania, Russian Federation, South Africa, Switzerland, Taiwan, USA, and Uruguay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2000, up to $50 million worth of food products were recalled due to the presence of <a href="/wiki/StarLink_corn_recall" title="StarLink corn recall">Starlink</a> genetically modified corn, which had been approved only for animal consumption and had not been approved for human consumption, and was subsequently withdrawn from the market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte#Origin_of_maize_and_interaction_with_teosintes" title="Teosinte">Origin of maize and interaction with teosintes</a></div>
<p>Maize is the domesticated variant of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">teosinte</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corn_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-corn-65">[65]</a></sup> The two plants have dissimilar appearance, maize having a single tall stalk with multiple leaves and teosinte being a short, bushy plant. The difference between the two is largely controlled by differences in just two genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corn_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-corn-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several theories had been proposed about the specific origin of maize in Mesoamerica:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilkes_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilkes-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyams1990_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hyams1990-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>It is a direct <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domestication</a> of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_people" title="Mexican people">Mexican</a> annual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">teosinte</a>, <i>Zea mays</i> ssp. <i>parviglumis</i>, native to the <a href="/wiki/Balsas_River" title="Balsas River">Balsas River</a> valley in south-eastern <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, with up to 12% of its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_material" title="Genetic material">genetic material</a> obtained from <i>Zea mays</i> ssp. <i>mexicana</i> through <a href="/wiki/Introgression" title="Introgression">introgression</a>. This theory was further confirmed by the 2002 study of Matsuoka et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mat_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mat-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li>It has been derived from hybridization between a small domesticated maize (a slightly changed form of a wild maize) and a teosinte of section <i>Luxuriantes</i>, either <i>Z. luxurians</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Zea_diploperennis" title="Zea diploperennis">Z. diploperennis</a></i>.</li>
<li>It has undergone two or more domestications either of a wild maize or of a teosinte. (The term "teosinte" describes all <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> and subspecies in the genus <i>Zea</i>, excluding <i>Zea mays</i> ssp. <i>mays</i>.)</li>
<li>It has evolved from a hybridization of <i>Z. diploperennis</i> by <i><a href="/wiki/Tripsacum_dactyloides" title="Tripsacum dactyloides">Tripsacum dactyloides</a></i>.</li></ol>
<p>In the late 1930s, Paul Mangelsdorf suggested that domesticated maize was the result of a hybridization event between an unknown wild maize and a species of <i><a href="/wiki/Tripsacum" title="Tripsacum">Tripsacum</a></i>, a related genus. This theory about the origin of maize has been refuted by modern <a href="/wiki/Genetic_testing" title="Genetic testing">genetic testing</a>, which refutes Mangelsdorf's model and the fourth listed above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilkes_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-wilkes-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>40</span></sup>
</p><p>The teosinte origin theory was proposed by the Russian botanist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikolai_Ivanovich_Vavilov" title="Nikolai Ivanovich Vavilov">Nikolai Ivanovich Vavilov</a> in 1931 and the later American <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a>-winner <a href="/wiki/George_Beadle" title="George Beadle">George Beadle</a> in 1932.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilkes_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-wilkes-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>10</span></sup> It is supported experimentally and by recent studies of the plants' genomes. Teosinte and maize are able to cross-breed and produce fertile offspring. A number of questions remain concerning the species, among them:
</p>
<ol><li>how the immense diversity of the species of sect. <i>Zea</i> originated,</li>
<li>how the tiny archaeological specimens of 3500–2700 BC could have been selected from a teosinte, and</li>
<li>how domestication could have proceeded without leaving remains of teosinte or maize with teosintoid traits earlier than the earliest known until recently, dating from ca. 1100 BC.</li></ol>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domestication</a> of maize is of particular interest to researchers—<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeologist" title="Archaeologist">archaeologists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">geneticists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethnobotany" title="Ethnobotany">ethnobotanists</a>, geographers, etc. The process is thought by some to have started 7,500 to 12,000 years ago. Research from the 1950s to 1970s originally focused on the hypothesis that maize domestication occurred in the highlands between the states of <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">Oaxaca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jalisco" title="Jalisco">Jalisco</a>, because the oldest archaeological remains of maize known at the time were found there.
</p>
<h3><span id="Connection_with_.27parviglumis.27_subspecies"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Connection_with_'parviglumis'_subspecies">Connection with 'parviglumis' subspecies</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maize-teosinte.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="643" data-file-width="1037" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Maize-teosinte.jpg/220px-Maize-teosinte.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Maize-teosinte.jpg/330px-Maize-teosinte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Maize-teosinte.jpg/440px-Maize-teosinte.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maize-teosinte.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">teosinte</a> (top), maize-teosinte hybrid (middle), maize (bottom)</div></div></div>
<p>Genetic studies, published in 2004 by <a href="/wiki/John_Doebley" title="John Doebley">John Doebley</a>, identified <i>Zea mays</i> ssp. <i>parviglumis</i>, native to the <a href="/wiki/Balsas_River" title="Balsas River">Balsas River</a> valley in Mexico's southwestern highlands, and also known as Balsas teosinte, as being the <a href="/wiki/Crop_wild_relative" title="Crop wild relative">crop wild relative</a> teosinte genetically most similar to modern maize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> This was been confirmed by further studies, which refined this hypothesis somewhat. Archaeobotanical studies, published in 2009, point to the middle part of the Balsas River valley as the likely location of early domestication; this river is not very long, so these locations are not very distant. Stone milling tools with maize residue have been found in an 8,700 year old layer of deposits in a cave not far from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iguala,_Guerrero" title="Iguala, Guerrero">Iguala, Guerrero</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ranere_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ranere-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mayan_-_Stucco_Portrait_Head_-_Walters_20092026_-_Three_Quarter_Right.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="1384" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Mayan_-_Stucco_Portrait_Head_-_Walters_20092026_-_Three_Quarter_Right.jpg/220px-Mayan_-_Stucco_Portrait_Head_-_Walters_20092026_-_Three_Quarter_Right.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Mayan_-_Stucco_Portrait_Head_-_Walters_20092026_-_Three_Quarter_Right.jpg/330px-Mayan_-_Stucco_Portrait_Head_-_Walters_20092026_-_Three_Quarter_Right.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Mayan_-_Stucco_Portrait_Head_-_Walters_20092026_-_Three_Quarter_Right.jpg/440px-Mayan_-_Stucco_Portrait_Head_-_Walters_20092026_-_Three_Quarter_Right.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mayan_-_Stucco_Portrait_Head_-_Walters_20092026_-_Three_Quarter_Right.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stucco head of the <a href="/wiki/Maya_maize_god" title="Maya maize god">Maya maize god</a>, 550–850 AD.</div></div></div>
<p>Doebley was part of the team that first published, in 2002, that maize had been domesticated only once, about 9,000 years ago, and then spread throughout the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mat_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mat-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>A primitive corn was being grown in southern Mexico, Central America, and northern South America 7,000 years ago. Archaeological remains of early maize ears, found at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guila_Naquitz_Cave" title="Guila Naquitz Cave">Guila Naquitz Cave</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca_Valley" title="Oaxaca Valley">Oaxaca Valley</a>, date back roughly 6,250 years; the oldest ears from caves near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tehuac%C3%A1n,_Puebla" title="Tehuacán, Puebla">Tehuacan</a>, Puebla, 5,450 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">B.P.</a> (Before Present)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archsouth_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-archsouth-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Maize pollen dated to 7,300 B.P. from <a href="/wiki/San_Andr%C3%A9s_(Mesoamerican_site)" title="San Andrés (Mesoamerican site)">San Andres, Tabasco</a>, on the Caribbean coast has also been recovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ranere_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ranere-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>As maize was introduced to new cultures, new uses were developed and new varieties selected to better serve in those preparations. Maize was the staple food, or a major staple – along with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(plant)" title="Squash (plant)">squash</a>, Andean region <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quinoa" title="Quinoa">quinoa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">beans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Amaranth" title="Amaranth">amaranth</a> – of most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian" title="Pre-Columbian">pre-Columbian</a> North American, Mesoamerican, South American, and Caribbean cultures. The Mesoamerican civilization, in particular, was deeply interrelated with maize. Its traditions and rituals involved all aspects of maize cultivation – from the planting to the food preparation. Maize formed the Mesoamerican people's identity.
</p><p>It is unknown what precipitated its domestication, because the edible portion of the wild variety is too small, and hard to obtain, to be eaten directly, as each kernel is enclosed in a very hard bivalve shell.
</p><p>In 1939, George Beadle demonstrated that the kernels of teosinte are readily "popped" for human consumption, like modern popcorn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Some have argued it would have taken too many generations of <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a> to produce large, compressed ears for efficient cultivation. However, studies of the hybrids readily made by intercrossing teosinte and modern maize suggest this objection is not well founded.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spreading_to_the_north">Spreading to the north</span></h3>
<p>Around 4,500 B.P., maize began to spread to the north; it was first cultivated in what is now the United States at several sites in New Mexico and Arizona, about 4,100 B.P.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the first millennium AD, maize cultivation spread more widely in the areas north. In particular, the large-scale adoption of maize agriculture and consumption in eastern North America took place about A.D. 900. Native Americans cleared large forest and grassland areas for the new crop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2005, research by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">USDA</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_Forest_Service" title="United States Forest Service">Forest Service</a> suggested that the rise in maize cultivation 500 to 1,000 years ago in what is now the southeastern United States corresponded with a decline of freshwater <a href="/wiki/Mussel" title="Mussel">mussels</a>, which are very sensitive to environmental changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivation">Cultivation</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planting">Planting</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="851" data-file-width="952" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG/220px-Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG/330px-Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG/440px-Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pl%C3%A0ntules_moresc_2012.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seedlings three weeks after sowing</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3008" data-file-width="2000" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg/170px-Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg/255px-Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg/340px-Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corn_Zea_mays_Plant_Row_2000px.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young stalks</div></div></div>
<p>Because it is cold-intolerant, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">temperate zones</a> maize must be planted in the spring. Its <a href="/wiki/Root_system" title="Root system">root system</a> is generally shallow, so the plant is dependent on soil moisture. As a plant that uses <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">C4 carbon fixation</a>, maize is a considerably more water-efficient crop than plants that use <a href="/wiki/C3_carbon_fixation" title="C3 carbon fixation">C3 carbon fixation</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Alfalfa" title="Alfalfa">alfalfa</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soybeans" title="Soybeans">soybeans</a>. Maize is most sensitive to drought at the time of silk emergence, when the flowers are ready for pollination. In the United States, a good harvest was traditionally predicted if the maize was "knee-high by the <a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(United_States)" title="Independence Day (United States)">Fourth of July</a>", although modern <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrids</a> generally exceed this growth rate. Maize used for <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">silage</a> is harvested while the plant is green and the fruit immature. Sweet corn is harvested in the "milk stage", after pollination but before starch has formed, between late summer and early to mid-autumn. Field maize is left in the field until very late in the autumn to thoroughly dry the grain, and may, in fact, sometimes not be harvested until winter or even early spring. The importance of sufficient soil moisture is shown in many parts of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, where periodic <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a> regularly causes maize crop failure and consequent <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>. Although it is grown mainly in wet, hot climates, it has been said to thrive in cold, hot, dry or wet conditions, meaning that it is an extremely versatile crop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maispflanze.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="480" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Maispflanze.jpg/170px-Maispflanze.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Maispflanze.jpg/255px-Maispflanze.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Maispflanze.jpg/340px-Maispflanze.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maispflanze.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mature plants showing ears</div></div></div>
<p>Maize was planted by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_American_(Americas)" title="Native American (Americas)">Native Americans</a> in hills, in a complex system known to some as the <a href="/wiki/Three_Sisters_(agriculture)" title="Three Sisters (agriculture)">Three Sisters</a>. Maize provided support for <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">beans</a>, and the beans provided nitrogen derived from nitrogen-fixing <a href="/wiki/Rhizobia" title="Rhizobia">rhizobia</a> bacteria which live on the roots of beans and other <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legumes</a>; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(fruit)" title="Squash (fruit)">squashes</a> provided ground cover to stop weeds and inhibit evaporation by providing shade over the soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mann_2011_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mann_2011-78">[78]</a></sup> This method was replaced by single species hill planting where each hill 60–120 cm (2.0–3.9 ft) apart was planted with three or four seeds, a method still used by home gardeners. A later technique was "checked maize", where hills were placed 40 in (1.0 m) apart in each direction, allowing cultivators to run through the field in two directions. In more arid lands, this was altered and seeds were planted in the bottom of 10–12 cm (3.9–4.7 in) deep furrows to collect water. Modern technique plants maize in rows which allows for cultivation while the plant is young, although the hill technique is still used in the maize fields of some Native American reservations. When maize is planted in rows, it also allows for planting of other crops between these rows to make more efficient use of land space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>In most regions today, maize grown in residential <a href="/wiki/Garden" title="Garden">gardens</a> is still often planted manually with a <a href="/wiki/Hoe_(tool)" title="Hoe (tool)">hoe</a>, whereas maize grown commercially is no longer planted manually but rather is planted with a <a href="/wiki/Planter_(farm_implement)" title="Planter (farm implement)">planter</a>. In North America, fields are often planted in a two-<a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">nitrogen-fixing</a> crop, often <a href="/wiki/Alfalfa" title="Alfalfa">alfalfa</a> in cooler climates and <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">soybeans</a> in regions with longer summers. Sometimes a third crop, <a href="/wiki/Winter_wheat" title="Winter wheat">winter wheat</a>, is added to the rotation.
</p><p>Many of the maize varieties grown in the United States and Canada are hybrids. Often the varieties have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetically_modified" title="Genetically modified">genetically modified</a> to tolerate <a href="/wiki/Glyphosate" title="Glyphosate">glyphosate</a> or to provide protection against natural pests. Glyphosate is an herbicide which kills all plants except those with genetic tolerance. This genetic tolerance is very rarely found in nature.
</p><p>In the midwestern United States, low-till or <a href="/wiki/No-till_farming" title="No-till farming">no-till farming</a> techniques are usually used. In low-till, fields are covered once, maybe twice, with a tillage implement either ahead of crop planting or after the previous harvest. The fields are planted and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anhydrous_ammonia" title="Anhydrous ammonia">fertilized</a>. <a href="/wiki/Weed" title="Weed">Weeds</a> are controlled through the use of <a href="/wiki/Herbicide" title="Herbicide">herbicides</a>, and no cultivation tillage is done during the growing season. This technique reduces moisture evaporation from the soil, and thus provides more moisture for the crop.
The technologies mentioned in the previous paragraph enable low-till and no-till farming. Weeds compete with the crop for moisture and nutrients, making them undesirable.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Harvesting">Harvesting</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:YellowCorn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2167" data-file-width="3032" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/YellowCorn.jpg/220px-YellowCorn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/YellowCorn.jpg/330px-YellowCorn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/YellowCorn.jpg/440px-YellowCorn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:YellowCorn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mature maize ears</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1110" data-file-width="1992" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg/220px-Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg/330px-Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg/440px-Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iowa_harvest_2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Harvesting maize, <a href="/wiki/Jones_County,_Iowa" title="Jones County, Iowa">Jones County, Iowa</a></div></div></div>
<p>Before the 20th century, all maize harvesting was by <a href="/wiki/Manual_labour" title="Manual labour">manual labour</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Grazing" title="Grazing">grazing</a>, or by some combination of those. Whether the ears were hand-picked and the <a href="/wiki/Corn_stover" title="Corn stover">stover</a> was grazed, or the whole plant was cut, gathered, and <a href="/wiki/Stook" title="Stook">shocked</a>, people and <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a> did all the work. Between the 1890s and the 1970s, the technology of maize harvesting expanded greatly. Today, all such technologies, from entirely manual harvesting to entirely mechanized, are still in use to some degree, <a href="/wiki/Appropriate_technology" title="Appropriate technology">as appropriate to each farm's needs</a>, although the thoroughly mechanized versions predominate, as they offer the lowest <a href="/wiki/Unit_cost" title="Unit cost">unit costs</a> when scaled to large farm operations. For small farms, their unit cost can be too high, as their higher <a href="/wiki/Fixed_cost" title="Fixed cost">fixed cost</a> cannot be <a href="/wiki/Amortization" title="Amortization">amortized</a> over as many units.
</p><p>Before <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, most maize in North America was harvested by hand. This involved a large numbers of workers and associated social events (husking or shucking <a href="/wiki/Communal_work" title="Communal work">bees</a>). From the 1890s onward, some machinery became available to partially mechanize the processes, such as one- and two-row mechanical pickers (picking the ear, leaving the <a href="/wiki/Corn_stover" title="Corn stover">stover</a>) and corn binders, which are <a href="/wiki/Reaper-binder" title="Reaper-binder">reaper-binders</a> designed specifically for maize (for example, <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=zA3zbJNvWuo" rel="nofollow">Video</a> on <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a>). The latter produce <a href="/wiki/Sheaf_(agriculture)" title="Sheaf (agriculture)">sheaves</a> that can be <a href="/wiki/Stook" title="Stook">shocked</a>. By hand or mechanical picker, the entire ear is harvested, which then requires a separate operation of a maize sheller to remove the kernels from the ear. Whole ears of maize were often stored in <a href="/wiki/Corn_crib" title="Corn crib">corn cribs</a>, and these whole ears are a sufficient form for some livestock feeding use. Today corn cribs with whole ears, and corn binders, are less common because most modern farms harvest the grain from the field with a <a href="/wiki/Combine_harvester" title="Combine harvester">combine</a> and store it in <a href="/wiki/Silo#Bins" title="Silo">bins</a>. The combine with a corn head (with points and snap rolls instead of a reel) does not cut the stalk; it simply pulls the stalk down. The stalk continues downward and is crumpled into a mangled pile on the ground, where it usually is left to become <a href="/wiki/Organic_matter" title="Organic matter">organic matter</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a>. The ear of maize is too large to pass between slots in a plate as the snap rolls pull the stalk away, leaving only the ear and husk to enter the machinery. The combine separates out the husk and the cob, keeping only the kernels.
</p><p>When maize is a <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">silage</a> crop, the entire plant is usually chopped at once with a <a href="/wiki/Forage_harvester" title="Forage harvester">forage harvester</a> (chopper) and ensiled in silos or polymer wrappers. Ensiling of sheaves cut by a corn binder was formerly common in some regions but has become uncommon.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MaizeYield.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1600" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/MaizeYield.png/220px-MaizeYield.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/MaizeYield.png/330px-MaizeYield.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/MaizeYield.png/440px-MaizeYield.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MaizeYield.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Worldwide maize production</div></div></div>
<p>For storing grain in bins, the moisture of the grain must be sufficiently low to avoid spoiling. If the moisture content of the harvested grain is too high, <a href="/wiki/Grain_drying" title="Grain drying">grain dryers</a> are used to reduce the moisture content by blowing heated air through the grain. This can require large amounts of energy in the form of combustible gases (<a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a> or <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>) and electricity to power the blowers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<p>Maize is widely cultivated throughout the world, and a greater weight of maize is produced each year than any other grain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In 2014, total world production was 1.04 billion <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, led by the United States with 35% of the total (table). China produced 21% of the global total.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Corn_production_in_the_United_States" title="Corn production in the United States">Corn production in the United States</a></div>
<p>In 2016, maize (corn) production was forecast to be over 15 billion <a href="/wiki/Bushel" title="Bushel">bushels</a>, an increase of 11% over 2014 American production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usda2016_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-usda2016-83">[83]</a></sup> Based on conditions as of August 2016, the expected yield would be the highest ever for the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usda2016_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-usda2016-83">[83]</a></sup> The area of harvested maize was forecast to be 87 million acres, an increase of 7% over 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usda2016_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-usda2016-83">[83]</a></sup> Maize is especially popular in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Midwest_United_States" title="Midwest United States">Midwestern</a> states such as <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>; in the latter, it was named the state's official grain in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ill_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-ill-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pests">Pests</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insects">Insects</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/African_armyworm" title="African armyworm">African armyworm</a> (<i>Spodoptera exempta</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eldana" title="Eldana">African sugarcane borer</a> (Eldana saccharina)</li>
<li>Common armyworm (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudaletia_unipuncta" title="Pseudaletia unipuncta">Pseudaletia unipuncta</a></i>)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_earwig" title="Common earwig">Common earwig</a> (<i>Forficula auricularia</i>)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corn_delphacid" title="Corn delphacid">Corn delphacid</a> (<i>Peregrinus maidis</i>)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corn_leaf_aphid" title="Corn leaf aphid">Corn leaf aphid</a> (<i>Rhopalosiphum maidis</i>)</li>
<li>Corn rootworms (<i><a href="/wiki/Diabrotica" title="Diabrotica">Diabrotica</a> spp</i>) including <a href="/wiki/Western_corn_rootworm" title="Western corn rootworm">Western corn rootworm</a> (<i>Diabrotica virgifera virgifera</i> LeConte), Northern corn rootworm (<i>D. barberi</i>) and Southern corn rootworm (<i>D. undecimpunctata howardi</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Euxesta_stigmatias" title="Euxesta stigmatias">Corn silkfly</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Euxesta_stigmatias" title="Euxesta stigmatias">Euxesta stigmatias</a></i>)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_corn_borer" title="Asian corn borer">Asian corn borer</a> (<i>Ostrinia furnacalis</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/European_corn_borer" title="European corn borer">European corn borer</a> (<i>Ostrinia nubilalis</i>) (ECB)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fall_armyworm" title="Fall armyworm">Fall armyworm</a> (<i>Spodoptera frugiperda</i>) Some sweet corn varieties have developed partial resistance to fall army worms by producing a unique 33-kD proteinase that significantly retards fall army worm growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></li>
<li>Corn earworm/Cotton bollworm (<i><a href="/wiki/Helicoverpa_zea" title="Helicoverpa zea">Helicoverpa zea</a></i>)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lesser_cornstalk_borer" title="Lesser cornstalk borer">Lesser cornstalk borer</a> (<i>Elasmopalpus lignosellus</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maize_weevil" title="Maize weevil">Maize weevil</a> (<i>Sitophilus zeamais</i>)</li>
<li>Northern armyworm, Oriental armyworm or Rice ear-cutting caterpillar (<i><a href="/wiki/Mythimna_separata" title="Mythimna separata">Mythimna separata</a></i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Southwestern_corn_borer" title="Southwestern corn borer">Southwestern corn borer</a> (<i>Diatraea grandiosella</i>)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stalk_Borer" title="Stalk Borer">Stalk borer</a> (<i>Papaipema nebris</i>)</li></ul>
<p>The susceptibility of maize to the European corn borer and corn rootworms, and the resulting large crop losses which are estimated at a billion dollars worldwide for each pest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> led to the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transgenic_plants" title="Transgenic plants">transgenics</a> expressing the <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_thuringiensis" title="Bacillus thuringiensis">Bacillus thuringiensis</a></i> toxin. "Bt maize" is widely grown in the United States and has been approved for release in Europe.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diseases">Diseases</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_maize_diseases" title="List of maize diseases">List of maize diseases</a></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Rust_(fungus)" title="Rust (fungus)">Rust</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Corn_smut" title="Corn smut">Corn smut</a> or common smut (<i>Ustilago maydis</i>): a fungal disease, known in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> as <i>huitlacoche</i>, which is prized by some as a gourmet delicacy in itself</li>
<li>Northern corn leaf blight <a class="external text" href="http://www.extension.purdue.edu/extmedia/BP/BP-84-W.pdf" rel="nofollow">(Purdue Extension site)</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.pioneer.com/home/site/us/agronomy/crop-management/corn-insect-disease/northern-leaf-blight" rel="nofollow">(Pioneer site)</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Southern_corn_leaf_blight" title="Southern corn leaf blight">Southern corn leaf blight</a></li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Maize_downy_mildew&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Maize downy mildew (page does not exist)">Maize downy mildew</a> (<i>Peronosclerospora</i> spp.)</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_food">Human food<span id="Food"></span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maisk%C3%B6rner.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3670" data-file-width="5496" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Maisk%C3%B6rner.jpg/220px-Maisk%C3%B6rner.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Maisk%C3%B6rner.jpg/330px-Maisk%C3%B6rner.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Maisk%C3%B6rner.jpg/440px-Maisk%C3%B6rner.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maisk%C3%B6rner.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vegetable maize (sweet corn)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_process_of_Maize_dish_making_1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/A_process_of_Maize_dish_making_1.JPG/220px-A_process_of_Maize_dish_making_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/A_process_of_Maize_dish_making_1.JPG/330px-A_process_of_Maize_dish_making_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/A_process_of_Maize_dish_making_1.JPG/440px-A_process_of_Maize_dish_making_1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_process_of_Maize_dish_making_1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Maize being <a href="/wiki/Roasting" title="Roasting">roasted</a> over an open flame in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sweet_White_Corn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="640" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Sweet_White_Corn.jpg/220px-Sweet_White_Corn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Sweet_White_Corn.jpg/330px-Sweet_White_Corn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Sweet_White_Corn.jpg/440px-Sweet_White_Corn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sweet_White_Corn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cut white sweet corn</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3044" data-file-width="3601" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png/220px-Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png/330px-Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png/440px-Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dried_Maize_Mote_from_Oaxaca.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dried maize <a href="/wiki/Mote_(food)" title="Mote (food)"><i>mote</i></a>, also known as <a href="/wiki/Hominy" title="Hominy">hominy</a>, is used in <a href="/wiki/Mexican_cuisine" title="Mexican cuisine">Mexican cuisine</a></div></div></div>
<p>Maize and <a href="/wiki/Cornmeal" title="Cornmeal">cornmeal</a> (ground dried maize) constitute a <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> in many regions of the world.
</p><p>Maize is central to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_food" title="Mexican food">Mexican food</a>. Virtually every dish in Mexican cuisine uses maize. In the form of grain or cornmeal, maize is the main ingredient of <a href="/wiki/Corn_tortilla" title="Corn tortilla">tortillas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamale" title="Tamale">tamales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pozole" title="Pozole">pozole</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atole" title="Atole">atole</a> and all the dishes based on them, like <a href="/wiki/Taco" title="Taco">tacos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quesadilla" title="Quesadilla">quesadillas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chilaquiles" title="Chilaquiles">chilaquiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enchilada" title="Enchilada">enchiladas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tostada_(tortilla)" title="Tostada (tortilla)">tostadas</a> and many more. In Mexico even a fungus of maize, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huitlacoche" title="Huitlacoche">huitlacoche</a> is considered a delicacy.
</p><p>Introduced into Africa by the Portuguese in the 16th century, maize has become Africa's most important staple food crop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Maize meal is made into a thick <a href="/wiki/Porridge" title="Porridge">porridge</a> in many cultures: from the <a href="/wiki/Polenta" title="Polenta">polenta</a> of Italy, the <i>angu</i> of Brazil, the <i><a href="/wiki/M%C4%83m%C4%83lig%C4%83" title="Mămăligă">mămăligă</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Mush_(cornmeal)" title="Mush (cornmeal)">cornmeal mush</a> in the US (and <a href="/wiki/Hominy" title="Hominy">hominy</a> <a href="/wiki/Grits" title="Grits">grits</a> in the South) or the food called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mealie_pap" title="Mealie pap">mealie pap</a> in South Africa and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sadza" title="Sadza">sadza</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nshima" title="Nshima">nshima</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ugali" title="Ugali">ugali</a></i> in other parts of Africa. Maize meal is also used as a replacement for <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> flour, to make <a href="/wiki/Cornbread" title="Cornbread">cornbread</a> and other baked products. <a href="/wiki/Masa" title="Masa">Masa</a> (cornmeal treated with <a href="/wiki/Limewater" title="Limewater">limewater</a>) is the main ingredient for <a href="/wiki/Tortilla" title="Tortilla">tortillas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atole" title="Atole">atole</a> and many other dishes of Central American food.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Popcorn" title="Popcorn">Popcorn</a> consists of kernels of certain varieties that explode when heated, forming fluffy pieces that are eaten as a snack. Roasted dried maize ears with semihardened kernels, coated with a seasoning mixture of fried chopped spring onions with salt added to the oil, is a popular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snack_food" title="Snack food">snack food</a> in Vietnam. <i>Cancha</i>, which are roasted maize chulpe kernels, are a very popular snack food in Peru, and also appears in traditional Peruvian <i><a href="/wiki/Ceviche" title="Ceviche">ceviche</a></i>. An unleavened bread called <i><a href="/wiki/Makki_di_roti" title="Makki di roti">makki di roti</a></i> is a popular bread eaten in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab region</a> of India and Pakistan.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Chicha" title="Chicha">Chicha</a></i> and <i>chicha morada</i> (purple chicha) are drinks typically made from particular types of maize. The first one is fermented and alcoholic, the second is a soft drink commonly drunk in Peru.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Corn_flakes" title="Corn flakes">Corn flakes</a> are a common <a href="/wiki/Breakfast_cereal" title="Breakfast cereal">breakfast cereal</a> in North America and the United Kingdom, and found in many other countries all over the world.
</p><p>Maize can also be prepared as <a href="/wiki/Hominy" title="Hominy">hominy</a>, in which the kernels are soaked with <a href="/wiki/Lye" title="Lye">lye</a> in a process called <a href="/wiki/Nixtamalization" title="Nixtamalization">nixtamalization</a>; or <a href="/wiki/Grits" title="Grits">grits</a>, which are coarsely ground hominy. These are commonly eaten in the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_United_States" title="Southeastern United States">Southeastern United States</a>, foods handed down from <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a>, who called the dish <a href="/wiki/Sagamite" title="Sagamite">sagamite</a>.
</p><p>The Brazilian dessert <a href="/wiki/Canjica_(dish)" title="Canjica (dish)"><i>canjica</i></a> is made by boiling maize kernels in sweetened milk.
</p><p>
Maize can also be harvested and consumed in the unripe state, when the kernels are fully grown but still soft. Unripe maize must usually be cooked to become palatable; this may be done by simply boiling or roasting the whole ears and eating the kernels right off the cob. <a href="/wiki/Sweet_corn" title="Sweet corn">Sweet corn</a>, a genetic variety that is high in sugars and low in starch, is usually consumed in the unripe state. Such <a href="/wiki/Corn_on_the_cob" title="Corn on the cob">corn on the cob</a> is a common dish in the United States, Canada, United Kingdom, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, some parts of South America, and the Balkans, but virtually unheard of in some European countries. Corn on the cob was hawked on the streets of early 19th-century New York City by poor, barefoot "<a href="/wiki/Hot_Corn" title="Hot Corn">Hot Corn</a> Girls", who were thus the precursors of <a href="/wiki/Hot_dog_cart" title="Hot dog cart">hot dog carts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Churro" title="Churro">churro</a> wagons, and fruit stands seen on the streets of big cities today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The cooked, unripe kernels may also be shaved off the cob and served as a <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetable</a> in side dishes, <a href="/wiki/Salad" title="Salad">salads</a>, <a href="/wiki/Garnish_(food)" title="Garnish (food)">garnishes</a>, etc. Alternatively, the raw unripe kernels may also be grated off the cobs and processed into a variety of cooked dishes, such as maize <a href="/wiki/Pur%C3%A9e" title="Purée">purée</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamale" title="Tamale">tamales</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pamonha" title="Pamonha">pamonhas</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Curau" title="Curau">curau</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Cake" title="Cake">cakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_cream" title="Ice cream">ice creams</a>, etc.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maizes_on_fire.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3020" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Maizes_on_fire.JPG/200px-Maizes_on_fire.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Maizes_on_fire.JPG/300px-Maizes_on_fire.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Maizes_on_fire.JPG/400px-Maizes_on_fire.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maizes_on_fire.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Maizes on fire</div></div></div>
<p>Maize is a major source of <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cornstarch" title="Cornstarch">Cornstarch</a> (maize flour) is a major ingredient in home cooking and in many industrialized food products. Maize is also a major source of <a href="/wiki/Cooking_oil" title="Cooking oil">cooking oil</a> (<a href="/wiki/Corn_oil" title="Corn oil">corn oil</a>) and of maize gluten. Maize starch can be <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolyzed</a> and enzymatically treated to produce syrups, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_fructose_corn_syrup" title="High fructose corn syrup">high fructose corn syrup</a>, a sweetener; and also fermented and distilled to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grain_alcohol" title="Grain alcohol">grain alcohol</a>. Grain alcohol from maize is traditionally the source of <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_whiskey" title="Bourbon whiskey">Bourbon whiskey</a>. Maize is sometimes used as the starch source for <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a>.
</p><p>Within the United States, the usage of maize for human consumption constitutes about 1/40th of the amount grown in the country. In the United States and Canada, maize is mostly grown to feed <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>, as forage, <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">silage</a> (made by fermentation of chopped green cornstalks), or grain. Maize meal is also a significant ingredient of some commercial animal food products, such as <a href="/wiki/Dog_food" title="Dog food">dog food</a>.
</p>
