<b>Quantity</b></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;font-weight:normal;padding-right:0.25em;">Water</th><td style="vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;">
75.96 g</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<hr/><div class="wrap" style="font-size:90%;padding:0.3em;line-height:1.2em;"><a class="external text" href="https://ndb.nal.usda.gov/ndb/search/list?qlookup=11167&amp;format=Full" rel="nofollow">Link to USDA Database entry</a><br/>One ear of medium size (6-3/4" to 7-1/2" long)<br/>maize has 90 grams of seeds</div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div class="plainlist" style="font-size:90%;background:#e0e0e0;padding:0.15em;line-height:1.25em;">
<ul><li>Units</li>
<li>μg = <a href="/wiki/Microgram" title="Microgram">micrograms</a> • mg = <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milligram" title="Milligram">milligrams</a></li>
<li>IU = <a href="/wiki/International_unit" title="International unit">International units</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><td class="wrap" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:90%;background:#e0e0e0;padding:0.3em;line-height:1.5em;font-weight:normal;">
<sup>†</sup>Percentages are roughly approximated using <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake" title="Dietary Reference Intake">US recommendations</a> for adults. <br/><span class="nowrap"><span style="font-size:85%;">Source: <a class="external text" href="https://ndb.nal.usda.gov/ndb/search/list" rel="nofollow">USDA Nutrient Database</a></span></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Raw, yellow, sweet maize kernels are composed of 76% water, 19% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a>, 3% <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, and 1% <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> (table). In a 100-<a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">gram</a> serving, maize kernels provide 86 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calories" title="Calories">calories</a> and are a good source (10-19% of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_Value" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a>) of the <a href="/wiki/B_vitamins" title="B vitamins">B vitamins</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thiamin" title="Thiamin">thiamin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niacin" title="Niacin">niacin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pantothenic_acid" title="Pantothenic acid">pantothenic acid</a> (B5) and <a href="/wiki/Folate" title="Folate">folate</a> (right table for raw, uncooked kernels, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USDA" title="USDA">USDA</a> Nutrient Database). In moderate amounts, they also supply <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_minerals" title="Essential minerals">essential minerals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> whereas other nutrients are in low amounts (table).
</p><p>Maize has suboptimal amounts of the <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid" title="Essential amino acid">essential amino acids</a> <a href="/wiki/Tryptophan" title="Tryptophan">tryptophan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lysine" title="Lysine">lysine</a>, which accounts for its lower status as a protein source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> The indigenous Americans overcame this deficiency with the inclusion of beans in their diet.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feed_and_fodder_for_livestock">Feed and fodder for livestock</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Corn_stover#Uses" title="Corn stover">Corn stover § Uses</a></div>
<p>Maize is a major source of both grain <a href="/wiki/Animal_feed" title="Animal feed">feed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fodder" title="Fodder">fodder</a> for <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>. It is fed to the livestock in various ways. When it is used as a grain crop, the dried <a href="/wiki/Corn_kernel" title="Corn kernel">kernels</a> are used as feed. They are often kept on the <a href="/wiki/Corncob" title="Corncob">cob</a> for storage in a <a href="/wiki/Corn_crib" title="Corn crib">corn crib</a>, or they may be shelled off for storage in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grain_bin" title="Grain bin">grain bin</a>. The farm that consumes the feed may produce it, purchase it on the market, or some of both. When the grain is used for feed, the rest of the plant (the <a href="/wiki/Corn_stover" title="Corn stover">corn stover</a>) can be used later as fodder, <a href="/wiki/Bedding_(animals)" title="Bedding (animals)">bedding</a> (litter), or <a href="/wiki/Soil_conditioner" title="Soil conditioner">soil amendment</a>. When the whole maize plant (grain plus stalks and leaves) is used for fodder, it is usually <a href="/wiki/Forage_harvester" title="Forage harvester">chopped all at once</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">ensilaged</a>, as digestibility and palatability are higher in the ensilaged form than in the dried form. Maize silage is one of the most valuable forages for ruminants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Before the advent of widespread ensilaging, it was traditional to gather the corn into <a href="/wiki/Stook" title="Stook">shocks</a> after harvesting, where it dried further. With or without a subsequent move to the cover of a barn, it was then stored for weeks to several months until fed to the livestock. Today ensilaging can occur not only in <a href="/wiki/Silo" title="Silo">siloes</a> but also in silage wrappers. However, in the tropics maize can be harvested year-round and fed as green forage to the animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemicals">Chemicals</span></h3>
<p>Starch from maize can also be made into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plastics" title="Plastics">plastics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fabric" title="Fabric">fabrics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adhesive" title="Adhesive">adhesives</a>, and many other chemical products.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Corn_steep_liquor" title="Corn steep liquor">corn steep liquor</a>, a plentiful watery byproduct of maize <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wet_milling" title="Wet milling">wet milling</a> process, is widely used in the <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemical industry</a> and research as a culture medium to grow many kinds of <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-a_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-a-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chrysanthemin" title="Chrysanthemin">Chrysanthemin</a> is found in <a href="/wiki/Purple_corn" title="Purple corn">purple corn</a> and is used as a food coloring.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bio-fuel">Bio-fuel</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Corn_ethanol" title="Corn ethanol">Corn ethanol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corn_stover" title="Corn stover">Corn stover</a></div>
<p>"Feed maize" is being used increasingly for heating;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> specialized <a href="/wiki/Pellet_stove" title="Pellet stove">corn stoves</a> (similar to <a href="/wiki/Wood_fuel" title="Wood fuel">wood stoves</a>) are available and use either feed maize or wood pellets to generate heat. Maize cobs are also used as a <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> fuel source. Maize is relatively cheap and home-heating furnaces have been developed which use maize kernels as a fuel. They feature a large hopper that feeds the uniformly sized maize kernels (or wood pellets or <a href="/wiki/Cherry" title="Cherry">cherry</a> pits) into the fire.
</p><p>Maize is increasingly used as a feedstock for the production of <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">ethanol fuel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> When considering where to construct an ethanol plant, one of the site selection criteria is to ensure there is locally available feedstock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Ethanol is mixed with gasoline to decrease the amount of pollutants emitted when used to fuel motor vehicles. High fuel prices in mid-2007 led to higher demand for ethanol, which in turn led to higher prices paid to farmers for maize. This led to the 2007 harvest being one of the most profitable maize crops in modern history for farmers. Because of the relationship between fuel and maize, prices paid for the crop now tend to track the price of oil.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The price of food is affected to a certain degree by the use of maize for biofuel production. The cost of transportation, production, and marketing are a large portion (80%) of the price of food in the United States. Higher energy costs affect these costs, especially transportation. The increase in food prices the consumer has been seeing is mainly due to the higher energy cost. The effect of biofuel production on other food crop prices is indirect. Use of maize for biofuel production increases the demand, and therefore price of maize. This, in turn, results in farm acreage being diverted from other food crops to maize production. This reduces the supply of the other food crops and increases their prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1349" data-file-width="1909" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG/220px-Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG/330px-Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG/440px-Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haase_anaerobic_digester.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Farm-based maize silage digester located near <a href="/wiki/Neum%C3%BCnster" title="Neumünster">Neumünster</a> in Germany, 2007. Green inflatable biogas holder is shown on top of the digester</div></div></div>
<p>Maize is widely used in Germany as a feedstock for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digesters" title="Anaerobic digesters">biogas plants</a>. Here the maize is harvested, shredded then placed in <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">silage</a> clamps from which it is fed into the biogas plants. This process makes use of the whole plant rather than simply using the kernels as in the production of fuel ethanol.
</p><p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomass_gasification" title="Biomass gasification">biomass gasification</a> power plant in Strem near <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCssing" title="Güssing">Güssing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burgenland" title="Burgenland">Burgenland</a>, Austria, began in 2005. Research is being done to make <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a> out of the biogas by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fischer_Tropsch" title="Fischer Tropsch">Fischer Tropsch</a> method.
</p><p>Increasingly, ethanol is being used at low concentrations (10% or less) as an additive in <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gasohol" title="Gasohol">gasohol</a>) for motor fuels to increase the <a href="/wiki/Octane_rating" title="Octane rating">octane rating</a>, lower pollutants, and reduce petroleum use (what is nowadays also known as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofuels" title="Biofuels">biofuels</a>" and has been generating an intense debate regarding the human beings' necessity of new sources of energy, on the one hand, and the need to maintain, in regions such as Latin America, the food habits and culture which has been the essence of civilizations such as the one originated in Mesoamerica; the entry, January 2008, of maize among the commercial agreements of <a href="/wiki/North_American_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="North American Free Trade Agreement">NAFTA</a> has increased this debate, considering the bad labor conditions of workers in the fields, and mainly the fact that NAFTA "opened the doors to the import of maize from the United States, where the farmers who grow it receive multimillion dollar subsidies and other government supports. (...) According to OXFAM UK, after NAFTA went into effect, the price of maize in Mexico fell 70% between 1994 and 2001. The number of farm jobs dropped as well: from 8.1 million in 1993 to 6.8 million in 2002. Many of those who found themselves without work were small-scale maize growers.").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> However, introduction in the northern latitudes of the US of <a class="external text" href="http://www.aces.uiuc.edu/news/stories/news4169.html" rel="nofollow">tropical maize for biofuels</a>, and not for human or animal consumption, may potentially alleviate this.
</p><p>As a result of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">US federal government</a> announcing its production target of 35 billion US gallons (130,000,000 m<sup>3</sup>) of biofuels by 2017, ethanol production will grow to 7 billion US gallons (26,000,000 m<sup>3</sup>) by 2010, up from 4.5 billion in 2006, boosting ethanol's share of maize demand in the US from 22.6 percent to 36.1 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commodity">Commodity</span></h3>
<p>Maize is bought and sold by investors and price speculators as a tradable commodity using corn <a href="/wiki/Futures_contract" title="Futures contract">futures contracts</a>. These "futures" are traded on the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Board_of_Trade" title="Chicago Board of Trade">Chicago Board of Trade</a> (CBOT) under <a href="/wiki/Ticker_symbol" title="Ticker symbol">ticker symbol</a> <b>C</b>. They are delivered every year in March, May, July, September, and December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ornamental_and_other_uses">Ornamental and other uses</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Corn_construction" title="Corn construction">Corn construction</a></div>
<p>Some forms of the plant are occasionally grown for ornamental use in the garden. For this purpose, variegated and colored leaf forms as well as those with colorful ears are used.
</p><p>Corncobs can be hollowed out and treated to make inexpensive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smoking_pipe_(tobacco)" title="Smoking pipe (tobacco)">smoking pipes</a>, first manufactured in the United States in 1869.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CornKernelBox.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/CornKernelBox.jpg/220px-CornKernelBox.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/CornKernelBox.jpg/330px-CornKernelBox.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/CornKernelBox.jpg/440px-CornKernelBox.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CornKernelBox.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Children playing in a maize kernel box</div></div></div>
<p>An unusual use for maize is to create a "<a href="/wiki/Corn_maze" title="Corn maze">corn maze</a>" (or "maize maze") as a tourist attraction. The idea of a maize maze was introduced by the American Maze Company who created a maze in <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> in 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Traditional mazes are most commonly grown using <a href="/wiki/Taxus" title="Taxus">yew</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hedge_(gardening)" title="Hedge (gardening)">hedges</a>, but these take several years to mature. The rapid growth of a field of maize allows a maze to be laid out using <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">GPS</a> at the start of a growing season and for the maize to grow tall enough to obstruct a visitor's line of sight by the start of the summer. In Canada and the US, these are popular in many farming communities.
</p><p>Maize kernels can be used in place of sand in a <a href="/wiki/Sandpit" title="Sandpit">sandboxlike</a> enclosure for children's play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corn_box_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-corn_box-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Stigmas from female maize flowers, popularly called <a href="/wiki/Corn_silk" title="Corn silk">corn silk</a>, are sold as <a href="/wiki/Herbalism" title="Herbalism">herbal supplements</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Maize is used as a <a href="/wiki/Bait_(luring_substance)" title="Bait (luring substance)">fish bait</a>, called "dough balls". It is particularly popular in Europe for <a href="/wiki/Coarse_fishing" title="Coarse fishing">coarse fishing</a>.
</p><p>Additionally, feed corn is sometimes used by hunters to bait animals such as deer or wild hogs.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_usage_breakdown">United States usage breakdown</span></h3>
<p>The breakdown of usage of the 12.1-billion-<a href="/wiki/Bushel" title="Bushel">bushel</a> (307-million-tonne) 2008 US maize crop was as follows, according to the World Agricultural Supply and Demand Estimates Report by the USDA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>

<p>In the US since 2009/2010, maize feedstock use for ethanol production has somewhat exceeded direct use for livestock feed; maize use for fuel ethanol was 5,130 million bushels (130 million tonnes) in the 2013/2014 marketing year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>A fraction of the maize feedstock dry matter used for ethanol production is usefully recovered as DDGS (dried distillers grains with solubles). In the 2010/2011 marketing year, about 29.1 million tonnes of DDGS were fed to US livestock and poultry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2011_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2011-108">[108]</a></sup> Because starch utilization in fermentation for ethanol production leaves other grain constituents more concentrated in the residue, the feed value per kg of DDGS, with regard to ruminant-metabolizable energy and protein, exceeds that of the grain. Feed value for monogastric animals, such as swine and poultry, is somewhat lower than for ruminants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2011_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2011-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>

<p><br/>
The following table shows the nutrient content of maize and major staple foods in a raw harvested form. Raw forms are not edible and cannot be digested. These must be sprouted, or prepared and cooked for human consumption. In sprouted or cooked form, the relative nutritional and anti-nutritional contents of each of these staples are different from that of raw form of these staples reported in the table below.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hazards">Hazards</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pellagra">Pellagra</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pellagra" title="Pellagra">Pellagra</a></div>
<p>When maize was first introduced into farming systems other than those used by traditional native-American peoples, it was generally welcomed with enthusiasm for its productivity. However, a widespread problem of malnutrition soon arose wherever maize was introduced as a <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a>. This was a mystery, since these types of malnutrition were not normally seen among the indigenous Americans, for whom maize was the principal staple food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pellagra_mystery_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-pellagra_mystery-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was eventually discovered that the indigenous Americans had learned to soak maize in <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkali</a>-water—made with ashes and lime (<a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">calcium oxide</a>) since at least 1200–1500 BC by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesoamericans" title="Mesoamericans">Mesoamericans</a> and North Americans—which liberates the B-vitamin <a href="/wiki/Niacin" title="Niacin">niacin</a>, the lack of which was the underlying cause of the condition known as <a href="/wiki/Pellagra" title="Pellagra">pellagra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StallerCarrasco2009_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-StallerCarrasco2009-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Maize was introduced into the diet of nonindigenous Americans without the necessary cultural knowledge acquired over thousands of years in the Americas. In the late 19th century, pellagra reached epidemic proportions in parts of the southern US, as medical researchers debated two theories for its origin: the deficiency theory (which was eventually shown to be true) said that pellagra was due to a deficiency of some nutrient, and the germ theory said that pellagra was caused by a germ transmitted by stable flies. A third theory, promoted by the eugenicist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Davenport" title="Charles Davenport">Charles Davenport</a>, held that people only contracted pellagra if they were susceptible to it due to certain "constitutional, inheritable" traits of the affected individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chase1980_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chase1980-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Once alkali processing and dietary variety were understood and applied, pellagra disappeared in the developed world. The development of high lysine maize and the promotion of a more balanced diet have also contributed to its demise. Pellagra still exists today in food-poor areas and refugee camps where people survive on donated maize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson2016_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson2016-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allergy">Allergy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Corn_allergy" title="Corn allergy">Corn allergy</a></div>
<p>Maize contains <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipid_transfer_proteins" title="Lipid transfer proteins">lipid transfer protein</a>, an indigestible protein that survives cooking. This protein has been linked to a rare and understudied <a href="/wiki/Allergy" title="Allergy">allergy</a> to maize in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The allergic reaction can cause skin rash, swelling or itching of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mucous_membranes" title="Mucous membranes">mucous membranes</a>, diarrhea, vomiting, <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a> and, in severe cases, <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">anaphylaxis</a>. It is unclear how common this allergy is in the general population.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:333px;max-width:333px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:177px;max-width:177px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mochica_Corn.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="295" data-file-width="258" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Mochica_Corn.jpg/175px-Mochica_Corn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/23/Mochica_Corn.jpg 1.5x" width="175"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Gold maize. Moche culture 300 A.D., <a href="/wiki/Larco_Museum" title="Larco Museum">Larco Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lima" title="Lima">Lima</a>, Peru</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CornWaterTower.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2304" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/CornWaterTower.JPG/150px-CornWaterTower.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/CornWaterTower.JPG/225px-CornWaterTower.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/CornWaterTower.JPG/300px-CornWaterTower.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Water_tower" title="Water tower">Water tower</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rochester,_Minnesota" title="Rochester, Minnesota">Rochester, Minnesota</a> being painted as an ear of maize</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<p>Maize has been an essential crop in the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a> since the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian era</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Moche_culture" title="Moche culture">Moche</a> culture from Northern <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> made ceramics from earth, water, and fire. This pottery was a sacred substance, formed in significant shapes and used to represent important themes. Maize was represented anthropomorphically as well as naturally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, maize ears along with tobacco leaves are carved into the capitals of columns in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a> building. Maize itself is sometimes used for temporary architectural detailing when the intent is to celebrate the fall season, local agricultural productivity and culture. Bundles of dried maize stalks are often displayed often along with pumpkins, gourds and straw in autumnal displays outside homes and businesses. A well-known example of architectural use is the <a href="/wiki/Corn_Palace" title="Corn Palace">Corn Palace</a> in Mitchell, South Dakota, which uses cobs and ears of colored maize to implement a mural design that is recycled annually. Another well known example is the <a href="/wiki/Field_of_Corn" title="Field of Corn">Field of Corn</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dublin,_Ohio" title="Dublin, Ohio">Dublin, Ohio</a>, where hundreds of concrete ears of corn lay in a grassy field.
</p><p>A maize stalk with two ripe ears is depicted on the <a href="/wiki/Obverse_and_reverse" title="Obverse and reverse">reverse</a> of the Croatian 1 <a href="/wiki/Croatian_kuna" title="Croatian kuna">lipa</a> coin, minted since 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140223100251/http://agron-www.agron.iastate.edu/Courses/agron212/readings/corn_history.htm" rel="nofollow">"Origin, History and Uses of Corn"</a>. <i>Iowa State University, Department of Agronomy</i>. February 11, 2014. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://agron-www.agron.iastate.edu/Courses/agron212/readings/corn_history.htm" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on February 23, 2014.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Iowa+State+University%2C+Department+of+Agronomy&amp;rft.atitle=Origin%2C+History+and+Uses+of+Corn&amp;rft.date=2014-02-11&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fagron-www.agron.iastate.edu%2FCourses%2Fagron212%2Freadings%2Fcorn_history.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMaize"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-ReferenceA-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_7-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_7-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_7-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Piperno, Dolores R. (2011). "The Origins of Plant Cultivation and Domestication in the New World Tropics: Patterns, Process, and New Developments". <i>Current Anthropology</i>. <b>52</b> (S4): 453–S470. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1086%2F659998" rel="nofollow">10.1086/659998</a>. <q>Recent studies in the Central Balsas River Valley of Mexico, maize’s postulated cradle of origin, document the presence of maize phytoliths and starch grains at 8700 BP, the earliest date recorded for the crop (Piperno et al. 2009; Ranere et al. 2009). A large corpus of data indicates that it was dispersed into lower Central America by 7600 BP and had moved into the inter-Andean valleys of Colombia between 7000 and 6000 BP. Given the number of Cauca Valley, Colombia, sites that demonstrate early maize, it is likely that the inter-Andean valleys were a major dispersal route for the crop after it entered South America</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Current+Anthropology&amp;rft.atitle=The+Origins+of+Plant+Cultivation+and+Domestication+in+the+New+World+Tropics%3A+Patterns%2C+Process%2C+and+New+Developments&amp;rft.volume=52&amp;rft.issue=S4&amp;rft.pages=453-S470&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1086%2F659998&amp;rft.aulast=Piperno&amp;rft.aufirst=Dolores+R.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMaize"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
